<p>Societies try a number of different measures to get as many people as possible into work, and various societies have experienced close to <a href="/wiki/Full_employment" title="Full employment">full employment</a> for extended periods, particularly during the <a href="/wiki/Post-World_War_II_economic_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-World War II economic expansion">Post-World War II economic expansion</a>. The United Kingdom in the 1950s and 60s averaged 1.6% unemployment,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> while in Australia the 1945 <i><a href="/wiki/White_Paper_on_Full_Employment_in_Australia" title="White Paper on Full Employment in Australia">White Paper on Full Employment in Australia</a></i> established a government policy of full employment, which policy lasted until the 1970s when the government ran out of money.</p>
<p>However, <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_economics" title="Mainstream economics">mainstream economic</a> discussions of <a href="/wiki/Full_employment" title="Full employment">full employment</a> since the 1970s suggest that attempts to reduce the level of unemployment below the <a href="/wiki/Natural_rate_of_unemployment" title="Natural rate of unemployment">natural rate of unemployment</a> will fail, resulting only in less output and more inflation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand-side_solutions">Demand-side solutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Demand-side solutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Increases in the demand for labour will move the economy along the demand curve, increasing wages and employment. The demand for labour in an economy is derived from the demand for goods and services. As such, if the demand for goods and services in the economy increases, the demand for labour will increase, increasing employment and wages.</p>
<p>There are many ways to stimulate demand for goods and services. Increasing wages to the working class (those more likely to spend the increased funds on goods and services, rather than various types of savings, or commodity purchases) is one theory proposed. Increased wages is believed to be more effective in boosting demand for goods and services than central banking strategies that put the increased money supply mostly into the hands of wealthy persons and institutions. Monetarists suggest that increasing money supply in general will increase short-term demand. Long-term the increased demand will be negated by inflation. A rise in fiscal expenditures is another strategy for boosting aggregate demand.</p>
<p>Providing aid to the unemployed is a strategy used to prevent cutbacks in consumption of goods and services which can lead to a vicious cycle of further job losses and further decreases in consumption/demand. Many countries aid the unemployed through <a href="/wiki/Social_welfare_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare program">social welfare program</a>s. These unemployment benefits include <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment insurance">unemployment insurance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_compensation" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment compensation">unemployment compensation</a>, welfare and subsidies to aid in retraining. The main goal of these programs is to alleviate short-term hardships and, more importantly, to allow workers more time to search for a job.</p>
<p>A direct demand-side solution to unemployment is government-funded employment of the able-bodied poor. This was notably implemented in Britain from the 17th century until 1948 in the institution of the <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a>, which provided jobs for the unemployed with harsh conditions and poor wages to dissuade their use. A modern alternative is a <a href="/wiki/Job_guarantee" title="Job guarantee">job guarantee</a>, where the government guarantees work at a living wage.</p>
<p>Temporary measures can include <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> programs such as the <a href="/wiki/Works_Progress_Administration" title="Works Progress Administration">Works Progress Administration</a>. Government-funded employment is not widely advocated as a solution to unemployment, except in times of crisis; this is attributed to the public sector jobs' existence depending directly on the tax receipts from private sector employment.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg/220px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg/330px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg/440px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1064" data-file-height="761" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Top_10%25.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Supply-side economics proposes that lower taxes lead to employment growth. Historical state data from the United States shows a heterogeneous result.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the U.S., the unemployment insurance allowance one receives is based solely on previous income (not time worked, family size, etc.) and usually compensates for one-third of one's previous income. To qualify, one must reside in their respective state for at least a year and, of course, work. The system was established by the <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_Act_of_1935" class="mw-redirect" title="Social Security Act of 1935">Social Security Act of 1935</a>. Although 90% of citizens are covered by unemployment insurance, less than 40% apply for and receive benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> However, the number applying for and receiving benefits increases during recessions. In cases of highly seasonal industries the system provides income to workers during the off seasons, thus encouraging them to stay attached to the industry.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg/220px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg/330px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg/440px-State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1065" data-file-height="714" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:State_Employment_growth_and_Tax_Changes_for_the_Bottom_90%25_v2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tax decreases on high income earners (top 10%) are not correlated with employment growth, however, tax decreases on lower income earners (bottom 90%) are correlated with employment growth.<sup id="cite_ref-economix.blogs.nytimes.com_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economix.blogs.nytimes.com-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economix.blogs.nytimes.com_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economix.blogs.nytimes.com-104">[104]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to classical economic theory, markets reach equilibrium where supply equals demand; everyone who wants to sell at the market price can. Those who do not want to sell at this price do not; in the labour market this is classical unemployment. Monetary policy and fiscal policy can both be used to increase short-term growth in the economy, increasing the demand for labour and decreasing unemployment.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supply-side_solutions">Supply-side solutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Supply-side solutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>However, the labor market is not 100% efficient: It does not clear, though it may be more efficient than bureaucracy. Some argue that minimum wages and union activity keep wages from falling, which means too many people want to sell their labour at the going price but cannot. This assumes <a href="/wiki/Perfect_competition" title="Perfect competition">perfect competition</a> exists in the labour market, specifically that no single entity is large enough to affect wage levels and that employees are similar in ability.</p>
<p>Advocates of <a href="/wiki/Supply-side" class="mw-redirect" title="Supply-side">supply-side</a> policies believe those policies can solve this by making the labour market more flexible. These include removing the minimum wage and reducing the power of unions. Supply-siders argue the reforms increase long-term growth by reducing labour costs. This increased supply of goods and services requires more workers, increasing employment. It is argued that supply-side policies, which include cutting taxes on businesses and reducing regulation, create jobs, reduce unemployment and decrease labour's share of national income. Other supply-side policies include education to make workers more attractive to employers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are relatively limited historical records on unemployment because it has not always been acknowledged or measured systematically. Industrialization involves economies of scale that often prevent individuals from having the capital to create their own jobs to be sef-employed. An individual who cannot either join an enterprise or create a job is unemployed. As individual farmers, ranchers, spinners, doctors and merchants are organized into large enterprises, those who cannot join or compete become unemployed.</p>
<p>Recognition of unemployment occurred slowly as economies across the world industrialized and bureaucratized. Before this, traditional <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self sufficient</a> native societies have no concept of unemployment. The recognition of the concept of "unemployment" is best exemplified through the well documented historical records in England. For example, in 16th century England no distinction was made between <a href="/wiki/Vagrancy_(people)" title="Vagrancy (people)">vagrants</a> and the jobless; both were simply categorized as "<a href="/wiki/Sturdy_beggar" title="Sturdy beggar">sturdy beggars</a>", to be punished and moved on.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The closing of the <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a> in the 1530s increased <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">church</a> had helped the poor. In addition, there was a significant rise in <a href="/wiki/Enclosure" title="Enclosure">enclosure</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor period</a>. Also the population was rising. Those unable to find work had a stark choice: starve or break the law. In 1535, a bill was drawn up calling for the creation of a system of <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> to deal with the problem of unemployment, to be funded by a tax on income and capital. A law passed a year later allowed vagabonds to be whipped and hanged.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1547, a bill was passed that subjected vagrants to some of the more extreme provisions of the criminal law, namely two years servitude and branding with a "V" as the penalty for the first offense and death for the second.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> During the reign of Henry VIII, as many as 72,000 people are estimated to have been executed.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In the 1576 Act each town was required to provide work for the unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Poor_Law_(1601)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan Poor Law (1601)">Elizabethan Poor Law of 1601</a>, one of the world's first government-sponsored welfare programs, made a clear distinction between those who were unable to work and those able-bodied people who refused employment.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law">Poor Law</a> systems of <a href="/wiki/English_Poor_Laws" title="English Poor Laws">England and Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Poor_Laws" title="Scottish Poor Laws">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Poor_Laws" title="Irish Poor Laws">Ireland</a> a <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a> was a place where people who were unable to support themselves, could go to live and work.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_Revolution_to_late_19th_century">Industrial Revolution to late 19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Industrial Revolution to late 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg/220px-Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg/330px-Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg/440px-Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3290" data-file-height="2156" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tompkins_square_riot_1874.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Depression_of_1873%E2%80%9379" class="mw-redirect" title="Depression of 1873–79">Depression of 1873–79</a>: <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police_Department" title="New York City Police Department">New York City police</a> violently attacking unemployed workers in <a href="/wiki/Tompkins_Square_Riot_(1874)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tompkins Square Riot (1874)">Tompkins Square Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> 1874</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Poverty was a highly visible problem in the eighteenth century, both in cities and in the countryside. In France and Britain by the end of the century, an estimated 10 percent of the people depended on charity or begging for their food.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Jackson J. Spielvogel (2008), Cengage Learning. p.566. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0495502871" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-495-50287-1</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>By 1776 some 1,912 parish and corporation workhouses had been established in England and Wales, housing almost 100,000 paupers.</p>
<p>A description of the miserable living standards of the mill workers in England in 1844 was given by <a href="/wiki/Fredrick_Engels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fredrick Engels">Fredrick Engels</a> in <i>The Condition of the Working-Class in England in 1844</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In the preface to the 1892 edition Engels notes that the extreme poverty he wrote about in 1844 had largely disappeared. <a href="/wiki/David_Ames_Wells" title="David Ames Wells">David Ames Wells</a> also noted that living conditions in England had improved near the end of the 19th century and that unemployment was low.</p>
<p>The scarcity and high price of labor in the U.S. during the 19th century was well documented by contemporary accounts, as in the following:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"The laboring classes are comparatively few in number, but this is counterbalanced by, and indeed, may be one of the causes of the eagerness by which they call in the use of machinery in almost every department of industry. Wherever it can be applied as a substitute for manual labor, it is universally and willingly resorted to ....It is this condition of the labor market, and this eager resort to machinery wherever it can be applied, to which, under the guidance of superior education and intelligence, the remarkable prosperity of the United States is due."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Whitworth" title="Joseph Whitworth">Joseph Whitworth</a>, 1854</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Scarcity of labor was a factor in the economics of slavery in the United States.</p>
<p>As new territories were opened and Federal land sales conducted, land had to be cleared and new homesteads established. Hundreds of thousands of immigrants annually came to the U.S. and found jobs digging canals and building railroads. Almost all work during most of the 19th century was done by hand or with horses, mules, or oxen, because there was very little mechanization. The workweek during most of the 19th century was 60 hours. Unemployment at times was between one and two percent.</p>
<p>The tight labor market was a factor in productivity gains allowing workers to maintain or increase their nominal wages during the secular deflation that caused real wages to rise at various times in the 19th century, especially in the final decades.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:320px;max-width:320px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:149px;max-width:149px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053,_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg/147px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg" width="147" height="219" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg/221px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg/294px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R79053%2C_Arbeitsloser_auf_Arbeitssuche.jpg 2x" data-file-width="532" data-file-height="792" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An unemployed <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a>, 1928. Unemployment in <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Germany</a> reached almost 30% of the workforce after the Great Depression.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:UnemployedMarch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UnemployedMarch.jpg/165px-UnemployedMarch.jpg" width="165" height="218" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UnemployedMarch.jpg/248px-UnemployedMarch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UnemployedMarch.jpg/330px-UnemployedMarch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="439" data-file-height="580" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Unemployed <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> men, marching for jobs during the Great Depression to Bathurst Street United Church, <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, 1930</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were labor shortages during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">WW I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jerome_1934_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jerome_1934-23">[23]</a></sup> Ford Motor Co. doubled wages to reduce turnover. After 1925 unemployment began to gradually rise.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Depression">Great Depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Great Depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a></div>
<p>The decade of the 1930s saw the Great Depression impact unemployment across the globe. One Soviet trading corporation in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York (state)">New York</a> averaged 350 applications a day from <a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">Americans</a> seeking jobs in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In Germany the unemployment rate reached nearly 25% in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some towns and cities in the north east of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, unemployment reached as high as 70%; the national unemployment level peaked at more than 22% in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Unemployment in Canada reached 27% at the depth of the Depression in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In 1929, the U.S. unemployment rate averaged 3%.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In 1933, 25% of all American workers and 37% of all nonfarm workers were unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WPA_Art_Poster,_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg/220px-WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg/330px-WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg/440px-WPA_Art_Poster%2C_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="445" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WPA_Art_Poster,_Work_Promotes_Confidence.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
WPA poster promoting the benefits of employment</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the U.S., the <a href="/wiki/Works_Progress_Administration" title="Works Progress Administration">Works Progress Administration</a> (1935–43) was the largest make-work program. It hired men (and some women) off the relief roles ("dole") typically for unskilled labor.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, the unemployment rate was 60%; in <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo, Ohio</a>, 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-Depression_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Depression-123">[123]</a></sup> There were two million <a href="/wiki/Homelessness_in_the_United_States" title="Homelessness in the United States">homeless people migrating across the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Depression_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Depression-123">[123]</a></sup> Over 3 million unemployed young men were taken out of the cities and placed into 2600+ work camps managed by the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unemployment in the United Kingdom fell later in the 1930s as the depression eased, and remained low (in six figures) after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</p>
<p>Fredrick Mills found that in the U.S., 51% of the decline in work hours was due to the fall in production and 49% was from increased productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Rifkin_1995_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rifkin_1995-125">[125]</a></sup> By 1972 <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unemployment in the United Kingdom">unemployment in the UK</a> had crept back up above 1,000,000, and was even higher by the end of the decade, with inflation also being high. Although the <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> economic policies of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher's</a> <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> government saw inflation reduced after 1979, unemployment soared in the early 1980s, exceeding 3,000,000&#160;– a level not seen for some 50 years&#160;– by 1982. This represented one in eight of the workforce, with unemployment exceeding 20% in some parts of the United Kingdom which had relied on the now-declining industries such as coal mining.<sup id="cite_ref-politics.co.uk_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politics.co.uk-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, this was a time of high unemployment in all major industrialised nations.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> By the spring of 1983, <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unemployment in the United Kingdom">unemployment in the United Kingdom</a> had risen by 6% in the previous 12 months; compared to 10% in Japan, 23% <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_in_the_United_States" title="Unemployment in the United States">in the United States of America</a> and 34% in <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> (seven years before <a href="/wiki/Reunification_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Reunification of Germany">reunification</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unemployment in the United Kingdom remained above 3,000,000 until the spring of 1987, by which time the economy was enjoying a boom.<sup id="cite_ref-politics.co.uk_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politics.co.uk-126">[126]</a></sup> By the end of 1989, unemployment had fallen to 1,600,000. However, inflation had reached 7.8% and the following year it reached a nine-year high of 9.5%; leading to increased interest rates.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_recession" title="Early 1990s recession">recession</a> began during 1990 and lasted until 1992. Unemployment began to increase and by the end of 1992 nearly 3,000,000 in the United Kingdom were unemployed. Then came a strong economic recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-politics.co.uk_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politics.co.uk-126">[126]</a></sup> With inflation down to 1.6% by 1993, unemployment then began to fall rapidly, standing at 1,800,000 by early 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unemployment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg/220px-Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg/330px-Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="432" data-file-height="322" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unemployment_Rate_of_Japan_1953-2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> unemployment rate, 1953–2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The official unemployment rate in the 16 <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> countries that use the euro rose to 10% in December 2009 as a result of <a href="/wiki/Late_2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late 2000s recession">another recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> had the highest unemployment rate in EU at 22.3% for November 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Europe's young workers have been especially hard hit.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In November 2009, the unemployment rate in the <a href="/wiki/EU27" class="mw-redirect" title="EU27">EU27</a> for those aged 15–24 was 18.3%. For those under 25, the unemployment rate in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> was 43.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Unemployment has risen in two-thirds of European countries since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Into the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unemployment in the United Kingdom">unemployment in the United Kingdom</a> remained low and the economy remaining strong, while at this time several other European economies&#160;– namely, France and Germany (reunified a decade earlier)&#160;– experienced a minor recession and a substantial rise in unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, when the recession brought on another increase in the United Kingdom, after 15 years of economic growth and no major rises in unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Early in 2009, unemployment passed the 2,000,000 mark, by which time economists were predicting it would soon reach 3,000,000.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> However, the end of the recession was declared in January 2010<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> and unemployment peaked at nearly 2,700,000 in 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> appearing to ease fears of unemployment reaching 3,000,000.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The unemployment rate of Britain's young black people was 47.4% in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> 2013/2014 has seen the employment rate increase from 1,935,836 to 2,173,012 as supported by<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> showing the UK is creating more job opportunities and forecasts the rate of increase in 2014/2015 will be another 7.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>An 26 April 2005 <i>Asia Times</i> article notes that, "In regional giant South Africa, some 300,000 textile workers have lost their jobs in the past two years due to the influx of Chinese goods".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The increasing <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States#International_trade" title="Economy of the United States">U.S. trade deficit</a> with China cost 2.4 million American jobs between 2001 and 2008, according to a study by the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Policy_Institute" title="Economic Policy Institute">Economic Policy Institute</a> (EPI).<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> From 2000 to 2007, the United States lost a total of 3.2 million manufacturing jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> 12.1% of US military veterans who had served after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001 were unemployed as of 2011; 29.1% of male veterans aged 18–24 were unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 25 million people in the world's 30 richest countries will have lost their jobs between the end of 2007 and the end of 2010 as the economic downturn pushes most countries into <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In April 2010, the U.S. unemployment rate was 9.9%, but the government's broader U-6 unemployment rate was 17.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> In April 2012, the unemployment rate was 4.6% in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In a 2012 news story, the <i>Financial Post</i> reported, "Nearly 75 million youth are unemployed around the world, an increase of more than 4 million since 2007. In the European Union, where a debt crisis followed the financial crisis, the youth unemployment rate rose to 18% last year from 12.5% in 2007, the ILO report shows."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
